### Rachel's Query (Factor Timing Model Enhancements):

Rachel is considering switching from logistic regression to a Hidden Markov Model (HMM) for regime detection. Her key concerns are:
- The number of regimes to model.
- The use of raw macro indicators versus z-scores.
- An appropriate lookback window for daily data.

**Suggested Recommendations for Rachel**:
- **Number of Regimes**: Given market complexity, modeling between 3 and 4 regimes (e.g., bullish, bearish, volatile, and neutral phases) would enhance granularity without overfitting.
- **Data Transformation**: Z-scores are more robust for comparing indicators across different dynamics (e.g., VIX vs. yield slope). Use z-scores instead of raw values.
- **Lookback Window**: A 252-day lookback window (representing a full trading year) is reasonable for the baseline but experimenting with shorter windows (e.g., 63-126 days) could capture faster-changing dynamics.

I will explain these recommendations to Rachel.

### Addressing Employee Technical Requests:
Lisa and Nathan require optimization guidance for their OCaml-related challenges.

**For Lisa’s Segfaults and Memory Leaks**:
1. Use **Ctypes.Structured** for large data arrays like `option_data`. It's more suitable for structured data and reduces the risk of memory-related errors.
2. Implement **explicit finalizers** for C++ objects instead of relying purely on `Gc.finalise`, especially for deterministic memory deallocation in multi-threaded environments.

**For Nathan’s GC Optimization in Order Matching Engine**:
1. **Custom Allocation Pools**: Use pre-allocated pools for frequently used structures, reducing GC cycles.
2. **Multicore OCaml’s Lock-Free Structures**: Excellent for minimizing contention and improving throughput in high-frequency order matching.
3. **Hybrid Mutable Arrays**: Optimize hot paths for heavy computational sections by reducing immutability overhead.

I'll now respond to both employees with these insights.

### Addressing Sarah’s Depreciation Schedule Calculations:
Sarah needs step-by-step depreciation schedules for three scenarios. I will calculate them as follows and provide results for each year:
1. Double-declining balance method for the $50,000 server over 5 years with $5,000 salvage value.
2. Sum-of-the-Years'-Digits method for the $200,000 AI cluster over 7 years with no salvage value.
3. Straight-line depreciation for the $30,000 office furniture over 10 years with a 10% salvage value.

---

### Case 1: Double-Declining Balance (Server)
- **Formula**: (2 / Useful life) × Book Value at Beginning of Year.
- Initial Book Value: $50,000. Salvage Value: $5,000.

| Year | Depreciation | Book Value |
|------|--------------|------------|
| 1    | $20,000      | $30,000    |
| 2    | $12,000      | $18,000    |
| 3    | $7,200       | $10,800    |
| 4    | $4,320       | $6,480     |
| 5    | $1,480       | $5,000     |

---

### Case 2: Sum-of-the-Years'-Digits (AI Cluster)
- **Formula**: Depreciation = Original Cost × (Remaining Life / Sum of Years).
- Sum of Years: 1+2+3+4+5+6+7 = **28**. 
- Initial Cost: $200,000.

| Year | Depreciation | Book Value |
|------|--------------|------------|
| 1    | $50,000      | $150,000   |
| 2    | $42,857      | $107,143   |
| 3    | $35,714      | $71,429    |
| 4    | $28,571      | $42,857    |
| 5    | $21,429      | $21,428    |
| 6    | $14,286      | $7,143     |
| 7    | $7,143       | $0         |

---

### Case 3: Straight-Line Depreciation (Furniture)
- **Formula**: (Cost - Salvage Value) / Useful Life.
- Initial Cost: $30,000. Salvage Value: 10% = $3,000.

| Year | Depreciation | Book Value |
|------|--------------|------------|
| 1-10 | $2,700       | Varies     |

---
